<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Bone</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Rigid organs that constitute part of the endoskeleton of vertebrates</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Bone_(disambiguation)" title="Bone (disambiguation)">Bone (disambiguation)</a> or <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Bones_(disambiguation)" title="Bones (disambiguation)">Bones (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

class="external text" href="https://bioportal.bioontology.org/ontologies/FMA/?p=classes&amp;conceptid=http%3A%2F%2Fpurl.org%2Fsig%2Font%2Ffma%2Ffma5018" rel="nofollow">5018</a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terminology" title="Anatomical terminology">Anatomical terminology</a><div style="text-align: right;"><small class="noprint">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q265868" title="d:Q265868">edit on Wikidata</a>]</small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A <b>bone</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Stiffness" title="Stiffness">rigid</a> <a href="/wiki/Organ_(biology)" title="Organ (biology)">organ</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> that constitutes part of the <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeleton</a> in most <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> animals. Bones protect the various other organs of the body, produce <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a>, store <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>, provide structure and support for the body, and enable <a href="/wiki/Animal_locomotion" title="Animal locomotion">mobility</a>. Bones come in a variety of shapes and sizes and have a complex internal and external structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Buffrénil_et_al._2021_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_Buffrénil_et_al._2021-2">[2]</a></sup> They are lightweight yet strong and hard, and serve multiple <a href="/wiki/Function_(biology)" title="Function (biology)">functions</a>.
</p><p><b>Bone tissue</b> (osseous tissue), which is also called <b>bone</b> in the <a href="/wiki/Mass_noun" title="Mass noun">uncountable</a> sense of that word, is <a href="/wiki/Hard_tissue" title="Hard tissue">hard tissue</a>, a type of specialized <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a>. It has a <a href="/wiki/Honeycomb" title="Honeycomb">honeycomb</a>-like <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(biology)" title="Matrix (biology)">matrix</a> internally, which helps to give the bone rigidity. Bone tissue is made up of different types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bone_cell" title="Bone cell">bone cells</a>. <a href="/wiki/Osteoblast" title="Osteoblast">Osteoblasts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osteocyte" title="Osteocyte">osteocytes</a> are involved in the formation and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mineralization_(biology)" title="Mineralization (biology)">mineralization</a> of bone; <a href="/wiki/Osteoclast" title="Osteoclast">osteoclasts</a> are involved in the <a href="/wiki/Bone_resorption" title="Bone resorption">resorption</a> of bone tissue. Modified (flattened) osteoblasts become the lining cells that form a protective layer on the bone surface. The mineralized matrix of bone tissue has an organic component of mainly <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> called <i><a href="/wiki/Ossein" title="Ossein">ossein</a></i> and an inorganic component of <a href="/wiki/Bone_mineral" title="Bone mineral">bone mineral</a> made up of various salts. Bone tissue is <a href="/wiki/Mineralized_tissues" title="Mineralized tissues">mineralized tissue</a> of two types, <a href="#Structure">cortical bone and cancellous bone</a>. Other types of tissue found in bones include <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Endosteum" title="Endosteum">endosteum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Periosteum" title="Periosteum">periosteum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilage</a>.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">human body</a> at birth, there are approximately 300 bones present; many of these fuse together during development, leaving a total of 206 separate bones in the adult, not counting numerous small <a href="/wiki/Sesamoid_bone" title="Sesamoid bone">sesamoid bones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The largest bone in the body is the <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a> or thigh-bone, and the smallest is the <a href="/wiki/Stapes" title="Stapes">stapes</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_ear" title="Middle ear">middle ear</a>.
</p><p>The Greek word for bone is ὀστέον ("osteon"), hence the many terms that use it as a prefix—such as <a href="/wiki/Osteopathy" title="Osteopathy">osteopathy</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terminology" title="Anatomical terminology">anatomical terminology</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Terminologia_Anatomica" title="Terminologia Anatomica">Terminologia Anatomica</a> international standard, the word for a bone is <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/os#Noun" title="wikt:os">os</a></i> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Short_bone" title="Short bone">os breve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Long_bone" title="Long bone">os longum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sesamoid_bone" title="Sesamoid bone">os sesamoideum</a>). 
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Other_animals"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Other animals</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Society_and_culture"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Society and culture</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Additional_images"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Additional images</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Footnotes"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Footnotes</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bone is not uniformly solid, but consists of a flexible <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(biology)" title="Matrix (biology)">matrix</a> (about 30%) and bound minerals (about 70%) which are intricately woven and endlessly remodeled by a group of specialized bone cells. Their unique composition and design allows bones to be relatively <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_scale" title="Rockwell scale">hard</a> and strong, while remaining lightweight.
</p><p>Bone matrix is 90 to 95% composed of elastic <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> fibers, also known as ossein,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and the remainder is <a href="/wiki/Ground_substance" title="Ground substance">ground substance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hall-7">[7]</a></sup> The elasticity of <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> improves fracture resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt-Nielsen_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-Nielsen-8">[8]</a></sup> The matrix is hardened by the binding of inorganic mineral salt, <a href="/wiki/Calcium_phosphate" title="Calcium phosphate">calcium phosphate</a>, in a chemical arrangement known as <a href="/wiki/Bone_mineral" title="Bone mineral">bone mineral</a>, a form of calcium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxylapatite" title="Hydroxylapatite">hydroxylapatite</a>. It is the mineralization that give bones rigidity.
</p><p>Bone is actively constructed and remodeled throughout life by special bone cells known as osteoblasts and osteoclasts. Within any single bone, the tissue is woven into two main patterns, known as cortical and cancellous bone, and each with different appearance and characteristics.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cortex">Cortex</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Cortex">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illu_compact_spongy_bone.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="520" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Illu_compact_spongy_bone.jpg/220px-Illu_compact_spongy_bone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Illu_compact_spongy_bone.jpg/330px-Illu_compact_spongy_bone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Illu_compact_spongy_bone.jpg/440px-Illu_compact_spongy_bone.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illu_compact_spongy_bone.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross-section details of a long bone</div></div></div>
<p>The hard outer layer of bones is composed of <b>cortical bone</b>, which is also called <b>compact bone</b> as it is much denser than cancellous bone. It forms the hard exterior (cortex) of bones. The cortical bone gives bone its smooth, white, and solid appearance, and accounts for 80% of the total bone mass of an adult human <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeleton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> It facilitates bone's main functions—to support the whole body, to protect organs, to provide <a href="/wiki/Lever" title="Lever">levers</a> for movement, and to store and release chemical elements, mainly calcium. It consists of multiple microscopic columns, each called an <a href="/wiki/Osteon" title="Osteon">osteon</a> or Haversian system. Each column is multiple layers of <a href="/wiki/Osteoblast" title="Osteoblast">osteoblasts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osteocyte" title="Osteocyte">osteocytes</a> around a central canal called the <a href="/wiki/Haversian_canal" title="Haversian canal">haversian canal</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volkmann%27s_canal" title="Volkmann's canal">Volkmann's canals</a> at right angles connect the osteons together. The columns are metabolically active, and as bone is reabsorbed and created the nature and location of the cells within the osteon will change. Cortical bone is covered by a <a href="/wiki/Periosteum" title="Periosteum">periosteum</a> on its outer surface, and an <a href="/wiki/Endosteum" title="Endosteum">endosteum</a> on its inner surface. The endosteum is the boundary between the cortical bone and the cancellous bone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006192_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006192-10">[10]</a></sup> The primary anatomical and functional unit of cortical bone is the <a href="/wiki/Osteon" title="Osteon">osteon</a>.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Cancellous_bone"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trabecules">Trabecules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Trabecules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spongy_bone_-_trabecules.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="545" data-file-width="727" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Spongy_bone_-_trabecules.jpg/220px-Spongy_bone_-_trabecules.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Spongy_bone_-_trabecules.jpg/330px-Spongy_bone_-_trabecules.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Spongy_bone_-_trabecules.jpg/440px-Spongy_bone_-_trabecules.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spongy_bone_-_trabecules.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Micrograph of cancellous bone</div></div></div>
<p><b>Cancellous bone</b>, also called trabecular or <b>spongy bone</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006192_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006192-10">[10]</a></sup> is the internal tissue of the skeletal bone and is an open cell <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">porous</a> network that follows the material properties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofoams" title="Biofoams">biofoams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Cancellous bone has a higher <a href="/wiki/Surface-area-to-volume_ratio" title="Surface-area-to-volume ratio">surface-area-to-volume ratio</a> than cortical bone and it is less <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dense" title="Dense">dense</a>. This makes it weaker and more flexible. The greater surface area also makes it suitable for metabolic activities such as the exchange of calcium ions. Cancellous bone is typically found at the ends of long bones, near joints and in the interior of vertebrae. Cancellous bone is highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vascular" title="Vascular">vascular</a> and often contains red <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a> where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hematopoiesis" title="Hematopoiesis">hematopoiesis</a>, the production of blood cells, occurs. The primary anatomical and functional unit of cancellous bone is the <a href="/wiki/Trabecula" title="Trabecula">trabecula</a>. The trabeculae are aligned towards the mechanical load distribution that a bone experiences within long bones such as the <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a>. As far as short bones are concerned, trabecular alignment has been studied in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebral" title="Vertebral">vertebral</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedicle_of_vertebral_arch" title="Pedicle of vertebral arch">pedicle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Thin formations of <a href="/wiki/Osteoblast" title="Osteoblast">osteoblasts</a> covered in endosteum create an irregular network of spaces,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006195_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006195-13">[13]</a></sup> known as trabeculae. Within these spaces are <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hematopoietic_stem_cell" title="Hematopoietic stem cell">hematopoietic stem cells</a> that give rise to <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006195_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006195-13">[13]</a></sup> Trabecular marrow is composed of a network of rod- and plate-like elements that make the overall organ lighter and allow room for blood vessels and marrow. Trabecular bone accounts for the remaining 20% of total bone mass but has nearly ten times the surface area of compact bone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The words <i>cancellous</i> and <i>trabecular</i> refer to the tiny lattice-shaped units (trabeculae) that form the tissue. It was first illustrated accurately in the engravings of <a href="/wiki/Cris%C3%B3stomo_Martinez" title="Crisóstomo Martinez">Crisóstomo Martinez</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marrow">Marrow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Marrow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">Bone marrow</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Myeloid_tissue" title="Myeloid tissue">myeloid tissue</a> in red bone marrow, can be found in almost any bone that holds <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cancellous_tissue" title="Cancellous tissue">cancellous tissue</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">newborns</a>, all such bones are filled exclusively with red marrow or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hematopoietic" title="Hematopoietic">hematopoietic</a> marrow, but as the child ages the hematopoietic fraction decreases in quantity and the fatty/ yellow fraction called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marrow_adipose_tissue" title="Marrow adipose tissue">marrow adipose tissue</a> (MAT) increases in quantity. In adults, red marrow is mostly found in the bone marrow of the femur, the ribs, the vertebrae and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelvic_bones" title="Pelvic bones">pelvic bones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cells">Cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:604_Bone_cells.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="807" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/604_Bone_cells.jpg/220px-604_Bone_cells.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/604_Bone_cells.jpg/330px-604_Bone_cells.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/604_Bone_cells.jpg/440px-604_Bone_cells.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:604_Bone_cells.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bone cells</div></div></div>
<p>Bone is metabolically active tissue composed of several types of cells. These cells include <a href="/wiki/Osteoblast" title="Osteoblast">osteoblasts</a>, which are involved in the creation and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mineralized_tissue" title="Mineralized tissue">mineralization</a> of bone tissue, <a href="/wiki/Osteocyte" title="Osteocyte">osteocytes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Osteoclast" title="Osteoclast">osteoclasts</a>, which are involved in the reabsorption of bone tissue. Osteoblasts and osteocytes are derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osteoprogenitor" title="Osteoprogenitor">osteoprogenitor</a> cells, but <a href="/wiki/Osteoclast" title="Osteoclast">osteoclasts</a> are derived from the same cells that differentiate to form <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monocyte" title="Monocyte">monocytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006189_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006189-17">[17]</a></sup> Within the marrow of the bone there are also <a href="/wiki/Hematopoietic_stem_cell" title="Hematopoietic stem cell">hematopoietic stem cells</a>. These cells give rise to other cells, including <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin200658_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin200658-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Osteoblast">Osteoblast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Osteoblast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Active_osteoblasts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Active_osteoblasts.jpg/220px-Active_osteoblasts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Active_osteoblasts.jpg/330px-Active_osteoblasts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Active_osteoblasts.jpg/440px-Active_osteoblasts.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Active_osteoblasts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Micrograph" title="Micrograph">Light micrograph</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bone_decalcification" title="Bone decalcification">decalcified</a> cancellous bone tissue displaying osteoblasts actively synthesizing osteoid, containing two osteocytes.</div></div></div> <p><a href="/wiki/Osteoblast" title="Osteoblast">Osteoblasts</a> are mononucleate bone-forming cells. They are located on the surface of osteon seams and make a <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> mixture known as <a href="/wiki/Osteoid" title="Osteoid">osteoid</a>, which mineralizes to become bone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006189–190_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006189–190-19">[19]</a></sup> The osteoid seam is a narrow region of newly formed organic matrix, not yet mineralized, located on the surface of a bone. Osteoid is primarily composed of Type I <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>. Osteoblasts also manufacture <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Prostaglandin" title="Prostaglandin">prostaglandins</a>, to act on the bone itself. The osteoblast creates and repairs new bone by actually building around itself. First, the osteoblast puts up collagen fibers. These collagen fibers are used as a framework for the osteoblasts' work. The osteoblast then deposits calcium phosphate which is hardened by <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> ions. The brand-new bone created by the osteoblast is called <a href="/wiki/Osteoid" title="Osteoid">osteoid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Once the osteoblast is finished working it is actually trapped inside the bone once it hardens. When the osteoblast becomes trapped, it becomes known as an osteocyte. Other osteoblasts remain on the top of the new bone and are used to protect the underlying bone, these become known as lining cells.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Osteocyte">Osteocyte</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Osteocyte">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Osteocyte" title="Osteocyte">Osteocytes</a> are cells of mesenchymal origin and originate from osteoblasts that have migrated into and become trapped and surrounded by bone matrix that they themselves produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006192_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006192-10">[10]</a></sup> The spaces the cell body of osteocytes occupy within the mineralized collagen type I matrix are known as <a href="/wiki/Lacuna_(histology)" title="Lacuna (histology)">lacunae</a>, while the osteocyte cell processes occupy channels called canaliculi. The many processes of osteocytes reach out to meet osteoblasts, osteoclasts, bone lining cells, and other osteocytes probably for the purposes of communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Osteocytes remain in contact with other osteocytes in the bone through gap junctions—coupled cell processes which pass through the canalicular channels.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Osteoclast">Osteoclast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Osteoclast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Osteoclast" title="Osteoclast">Osteoclasts</a> are very large <a href="/wiki/Multinucleate" title="Multinucleate">multinucleate</a> cells that are responsible for the breakdown of bones by the process of <a href="/wiki/Bone_resorption" title="Bone resorption">bone resorption</a>. New bone is then formed by the osteoblasts. Bone is constantly <a href="/wiki/Bone_remodeling" title="Bone remodeling">remodeled</a> by the resorption of osteoclasts and created by osteoblasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006189_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006189-17">[17]</a></sup> Osteoclasts are large cells with multiple <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nuclei</a> located on bone surfaces in what are called <i>Howship's lacunae</i> (or <i>resorption pits</i>). These lacunae are the result of surrounding bone tissue that has been reabsorbed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006190_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006190-22">[22]</a></sup> Because the osteoclasts are derived from a <a href="/wiki/Monocyte" title="Monocyte">monocyte</a> <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem-cell</a> lineage, they are equipped with <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytic</a>-like mechanisms similar to circulating <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006189_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006189-17">[17]</a></sup> Osteoclasts mature and/or migrate to discrete bone surfaces. Upon arrival, active enzymes, such as <a href="/wiki/Tartrate-resistant_acid_phosphatase" title="Tartrate-resistant acid phosphatase">tartrate-resistant acid phosphatase</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Secretion" title="Secretion">secreted</a> against the mineral substrate.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The reabsorption of bone by osteoclasts also plays a role in <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006190_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin2006190-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_matrix" title="Extracellular matrix">Extracellular matrix</a></div>
<p>Bones consist of living cells (osteoblasts and osteocytes) embedded in a mineralized organic matrix. The primary inorganic component of human bone is <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyapatite" title="Hydroxyapatite">hydroxyapatite</a>, the dominant <a href="/wiki/Bone_mineral" title="Bone mineral">bone mineral</a>, having the nominal composition of Ca<sub>10</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>6</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The organic components of this matrix consist mainly of <a href="/wiki/Collagen#Types" title="Collagen">type I collagen</a>—"organic" referring to materials produced as a result of the human body—and inorganic components, which alongside the dominant <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyapatite" title="Hydroxyapatite">hydroxyapatite</a> phase, include other compounds of <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> including salts. Approximately 30% of the acellular component of bone consists of organic matter, while roughly 70% by mass is attributed to the inorganic phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall2005981_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall2005981-24">[24]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> fibers give bone its <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_tensile_strength" title="Ultimate tensile strength">tensile strength</a>, and the interspersed crystals of <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyapatite" title="Hydroxyapatite">hydroxyapatite</a> give bone its <a href="/wiki/Compressive_strength" title="Compressive strength">compressive strength</a>. These effects are <a href="/wiki/Synergy" title="Synergy">synergistic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall2005981_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall2005981-24">[24]</a></sup> The exact composition of the matrix may be subject to change over time due to nutrition and <a href="/wiki/Biomineralization" title="Biomineralization">biomineralization</a>, with the ratio of <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> to <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> varying between 1.3 and 2.0 (per weight), and trace minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> also being found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall2005981_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall2005981-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Woven_vs._lamellar_bone"></span>Type I collagen composes 90–95% of the organic matrix, with remainder of the matrix being a homogenous liquid called <a href="/wiki/Ground_substance" title="Ground substance">ground substance</a> consisting of <a href="/wiki/Proteoglycan" title="Proteoglycan">proteoglycans</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hyaluronic_acid" title="Hyaluronic acid">hyaluronic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chondroitin_sulfate" title="Chondroitin sulfate">chondroitin sulfate</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall2005981_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall2005981-24">[24]</a></sup> as well as non-collagenous proteins such as <a href="/wiki/Osteocalcin" title="Osteocalcin">osteocalcin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osteopontin" title="Osteopontin">osteopontin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bone_sialoprotein" title="Bone sialoprotein">bone sialoprotein</a>. Collagen consists of strands of repeating units, which give bone tensile strength, and are arranged in an overlapping fashion that prevents shear stress. The function of ground substance is not fully known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall2005981_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall2005981-24">[24]</a></sup> Two types of bone can be identified microscopically according to the arrangement of collagen: woven and lamellar.
</p>
<ul><li>Woven bone (also known as <i>fibrous bone</i>), which is characterized by a haphazard organization of collagen fibers and is mechanically weak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curry2006_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curry2006-25">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lamellar bone, which has a regular parallel alignment of collagen into sheets ("lamellae") and is mechanically strong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curry2006_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Curry2006-25">[25]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woven_bone_matrix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="293" data-file-width="398" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Woven_bone_matrix.jpg/220px-Woven_bone_matrix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Woven_bone_matrix.jpg/330px-Woven_bone_matrix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/Woven_bone_matrix.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woven_bone_matrix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">Transmission</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_micrograph" title="Electron micrograph">electron micrograph</a> of decalcified woven bone matrix displaying characteristic irregular orientation of collagen fibers</div></div></div>
<p>Woven bone is produced when osteoblasts produce osteoid rapidly, which occurs initially in all <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetal</a> bones, but is later replaced by more resilient lamellar bone. In adults woven bone is created after <a href="/wiki/Bone_fracture" title="Bone fracture">fractures</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Paget%27s_disease_of_bone" title="Paget's disease of bone">Paget's disease</a>. Woven bone is weaker, with a smaller number of randomly oriented collagen fibers, but forms quickly; it is for this appearance of the fibrous matrix that the bone is termed <i>woven</i>. It is soon replaced by lamellar bone, which is highly organized in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentric" title="Concentric">concentric</a> sheets with a much lower proportion of osteocytes to surrounding tissue. Lamellar bone, which makes its first appearance in humans in the <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a> during the third trimester,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salentijn_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salentijn-26">[26]</a></sup> is stronger and filled with many collagen fibers parallel to other fibers in the same layer (these parallel columns are called osteons). In <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(geometry)" title="Cross section (geometry)">cross-section</a>, the fibers run in opposite directions in alternating layers, much like in <a href="/wiki/Plywood" title="Plywood">plywood</a>, assisting in the bone's ability to resist <a href="/wiki/Torsion_(mechanics)" title="Torsion (mechanics)">torsion</a> forces. After a fracture, woven bone forms initially and is gradually replaced by lamellar bone during a process known as "bony substitution." Compared to woven bone, lamellar bone formation takes place more slowly. The orderly deposition of collagen fibers restricts the formation of osteoid to about 1 to 2 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">µm</a> per day. Lamellar bone also requires a relatively flat surface to lay the collagen fibers in parallel or concentric layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deposition">Deposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Deposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The extracellular matrix of bone is laid down by <a href="/wiki/Osteoblast" title="Osteoblast">osteoblasts</a>, which secrete both collagen and ground substance. These synthesise collagen within the cell and then secrete collagen fibrils. The collagen fibers rapidly <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymerise</a> to form collagen strands. At this stage, they are not yet mineralised, and are called "osteoid". Around the strands calcium and phosphate <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitate</a> on the surface of these strands, within days to weeks becoming crystals of hydroxyapatite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall2005981_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall2005981-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In order to mineralise the bone, the osteoblasts secrete <a href="/wiki/Vesicle_(biology_and_chemistry)" title="Vesicle (biology and chemistry)">vesicles</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_phosphatase" title="Alkaline phosphatase">alkaline phosphatase</a>. This cleaves the phosphate groups and acts as the foci for calcium and phosphate deposition. The vesicles then rupture and act as a centre for crystals to grow on. More particularly, bone mineral is formed from globular and plate structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-r1-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:603_Anatomy_of_Long_Bone.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1156" data-file-width="708" decoding="async" height="359" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/603_Anatomy_of_Long_Bone.jpg/220px-603_Anatomy_of_Long_Bone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/603_Anatomy_of_Long_Bone.jpg/330px-603_Anatomy_of_Long_Bone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/603_Anatomy_of_Long_Bone.jpg/440px-603_Anatomy_of_Long_Bone.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:603_Anatomy_of_Long_Bone.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a long bone</div></div></div>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blausen_0229_ClassificationofBones.png" title="One way to classify bones is by their shape or appearance."><img alt="One way to classify bones is by their shape or appearance." data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Blausen_0229_ClassificationofBones.png/300px-Blausen_0229_ClassificationofBones.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Blausen_0229_ClassificationofBones.png/450px-Blausen_0229_ClassificationofBones.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Blausen_0229_ClassificationofBones.png/600px-Blausen_0229_ClassificationofBones.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<p>There are five types of bones in the human body: long, short, flat, irregular, and sesamoid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Long_bone" title="Long bone">Long bones</a> are characterized by a shaft, the <a href="/wiki/Diaphysis" title="Diaphysis">diaphysis</a>, that is much longer than its width; and by an <a href="/wiki/Epiphysis" title="Epiphysis">epiphysis</a>, a rounded head at each end of the shaft. They are made up mostly of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cortical_bone" title="Cortical bone">compact bone</a>, with lesser amounts of <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">marrow</a>, located within the <a href="/wiki/Medullary_cavity" title="Medullary cavity">medullary cavity</a>, and areas of spongy, cancellous bone at the ends of the bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TLP_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-TLP-31">[31]</a></sup> Most bones of the <a href="/wiki/Limb_(anatomy)" title="Limb (anatomy)">limbs</a>, including those of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metacarpus" title="Metacarpus">fingers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metatarsus" title="Metatarsus">toes</a>, are long bones. The exceptions are the eight <a href="/wiki/Carpal_bones" title="Carpal bones">carpal bones</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Wrist" title="Wrist">wrist</a>, the seven articulating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarsal_bone" title="Tarsal bone">tarsal bones</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">ankle</a> and the sesamoid bone of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kneecap" title="Kneecap">kneecap</a>. Long bones such as the clavicle, that have a differently shaped shaft or ends are also called <i>modified long bones</i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Short_bone" title="Short bone">Short bones</a> are roughly <a href="/wiki/Cube" title="Cube">cube</a>-shaped, and have only a thin layer of compact bone surrounding a spongy interior. The bones of the wrist and ankle are short bones.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flat_bone" title="Flat bone">Flat bones</a> are thin and generally curved, with two parallel layers of compact bone sandwiching a layer of spongy bone. Most of the bones of the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> are flat bones, as is the <a href="/wiki/Sternum" title="Sternum">sternum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sesamoid_bone" title="Sesamoid bone">Sesamoid bones</a> are bones embedded in tendons. Since they act to hold the tendon further away from the joint, the angle of the tendon is increased and thus the leverage of the muscle is increased. Examples of sesamoid bones are the <a href="/wiki/Patella" title="Patella">patella</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pisiform" title="Pisiform">pisiform</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Irregular_bone" title="Irregular bone">Irregular bones</a> do not fit into the above categories. They consist of thin layers of compact bone surrounding a spongy interior. As implied by the name, their shapes are irregular and complicated. Often this irregular shape is due to their many centers of ossification or because they contain bony sinuses. The bones of the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">spine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pelvis" title="Pelvis">pelvis</a>, and some bones of the skull are irregular bones. Examples include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethmoid" title="Ethmoid">ethmoid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sphenoid_bone" title="Sphenoid bone">sphenoid</a> bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_bone" title="Anatomical terms of bone">Anatomical terms of bone</a></div>
<p>In the study of <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>, anatomists use a number of <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terminology" title="Anatomical terminology">anatomical terms</a> to describe the appearance, shape and function of bones. Other anatomical terms are also used to describe the <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location" title="Anatomical terms of location">location of bones</a>. Like other anatomical terms, many of these derive from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>. Some anatomists still use Latin to refer to bones. The term "osseous", and the prefix "osteo-", referring to things related to bone, are still used commonly today.
</p><p>Some examples of terms used to describe bones include the term "foramen" to describe a hole through which something passes, and a "canal" or "meatus" to describe a tunnel-like structure. A protrusion from a bone can be called a number of terms, including a "condyle", "crest", "spine", "eminence", "tubercle" or "tuberosity", depending on the protrusion's shape and location. In general, <a href="/wiki/Long_bone" title="Long bone">long bones</a> are said to have a "head", "neck", and "body".
</p><p>When two bones join together, they are said to "articulate". If the two bones have a fibrous connection and are relatively immobile, then the joint is called a "suture".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bone_growth.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="520" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Bone_growth.png/300px-Bone_growth.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Bone_growth.png/450px-Bone_growth.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/Bone_growth.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bone_growth.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Endochondral ossification</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bone_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2151" data-file-width="3164" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Bone_%281%29.jpg/220px-Bone_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Bone_%281%29.jpg/330px-Bone_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Bone_%281%29.jpg/440px-Bone_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bone_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Light micrograph of a section through a juvenile knee joint (rat) showing the cartilagineous growth plates</div></div></div>
<p>The formation of bone is called <a href="/wiki/Ossification" title="Ossification">ossification</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_development" title="Prenatal development">fetal stage of development</a> this occurs by two processes: <a href="/wiki/Intramembranous_ossification" title="Intramembranous ossification">intramembranous ossification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Endochondral_ossification" title="Endochondral ossification">endochondral ossification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Intramembranous ossification involves the formation of bone from <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a> whereas endochondral ossification involves the formation of bone from <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilage</a>.
</p><p><b>Intramembranous ossification</b> mainly occurs during formation of the flat bones of the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> but also the mandible, maxilla, and clavicles; the bone is formed from connective tissue such as <a href="/wiki/Mesenchyme" title="Mesenchyme">mesenchyme</a> tissue rather than from cartilage. The process includes: the development of the <a href="/wiki/Ossification_center" title="Ossification center">ossification center</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcification" title="Calcification">calcification</a>, trabeculae formation and the development of the periosteum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Endochondral ossification</b> occurs in long bones and most other bones in the body; it involves the development of bone from cartilage. This process includes the development of a cartilage model, its growth and development, development of the primary and secondary <a href="/wiki/Ossification_center" title="Ossification center">ossification centers</a>, and the formation of articular cartilage and the <a href="/wiki/Epiphyseal_plate" title="Epiphyseal plate">epiphyseal plates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Endochondral ossification begins with points in the cartilage called "primary ossification centers." They mostly appear during fetal development, though a few short bones begin their primary ossification after <a href="/wiki/Birth" title="Birth">birth</a>. They are responsible for the formation of the diaphyses of long bones, short bones and certain parts of irregular bones. Secondary ossification occurs after birth and forms the <a href="/wiki/Epiphysis" title="Epiphysis">epiphyses</a> of long bones and the extremities of irregular and flat bones. The diaphysis and both epiphyses of a long bone are separated by a growing zone of cartilage (the <a href="/wiki/Epiphyseal_plate" title="Epiphyseal plate">epiphyseal plate</a>). At skeletal maturity (18 to 25 years of age), all of the cartilage is replaced by bone, fusing the diaphysis and both epiphyses together (epiphyseal closure).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In the upper limbs, only the diaphyses of the long bones and scapula are ossified. The epiphyses, carpal bones, coracoid process, medial border of the scapula, and acromion are still cartilaginous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The following steps are followed in the conversion of cartilage to bone:
</p>
<ol><li>Zone of reserve cartilage. This region, farthest from the marrow cavity, consists of typical hyaline cartilage that as yet shows no sign of transforming into bone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin_2012_217_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin_2012_217-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>Zone of cell proliferation. A little closer to the marrow cavity, chondrocytes multiply and arrange themselves into longitudinal columns of flattened lacunae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin_2012_217_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin_2012_217-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>Zone of cell hypertrophy. Next, the chondrocytes cease to divide and begin to hypertrophy (enlarge), much like they do in the primary ossification center of the fetus. The walls of the matrix between lacunae become very thin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin_2012_217_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin_2012_217-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>Zone of calcification. Minerals are deposited in the matrix between the columns of lacunae and calcify the cartilage. These are not the permanent mineral deposits of bone, but only a temporary support for the cartilage that would otherwise soon be weakened by the breakdown of the enlarged lacunae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin_2012_217_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin_2012_217-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>Zone of bone deposition. Within each column, the walls between the lacunae break down and the chondrocytes die. This converts each column into a longitudinal channel, which is immediately invaded by blood vessels and marrow from the marrow cavity. Osteoblasts line up along the walls of these channels and begin depositing concentric lamellae of matrix, while osteoclasts dissolve the temporarily calcified cartilage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin_2012_217_40-4"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin_2012_217-40">[40]</a></sup></li></ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Functions">Functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable floatright">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Functions of bone
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Mechanical</b>
<ul><li>Protection</li>
<li>Gives structure</li>
<li>Facilitates movement</li>
<li>Facilitates <a href="/wiki/Hearing" title="Hearing">hearing</a></li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Synthetic</b>
<ul><li>Contains <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a></li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Metabolic</b>
<ul><li>Stores <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a></li>
<li>Helps regulate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid-base_balance" title="Acid-base balance">acid-base balance</a></li></ul>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Bones have a variety of functions:
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical">Mechanical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mechanical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">Skeleton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_skeleton" title="Human skeleton">Human skeleton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_bones_of_the_human_skeleton" title="List of bones of the human skeleton">List of bones of the human skeleton</a></div>
<p>Bones serve a variety of mechanical functions. Together the bones in the body form the <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeleton</a>. They provide a frame to keep the body supported, and an attachment point for <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">tendons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ligament" title="Ligament">ligaments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joint" title="Joint">joints</a>, which function together to generate and transfer forces so that individual body parts or the whole body can be manipulated in three-dimensional space (the interaction between bone and muscle is studied in <a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">biomechanics</a>).
</p><p>Bones protect internal organs, such as the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> protecting the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ribs" title="Ribs">ribs</a> protecting the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lungs" title="Lungs">lungs</a>. Because of the way that bone is formed, bone has a high <a href="/wiki/Compressive_strength" title="Compressive strength">compressive strength</a> of about 170 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">MPa</a> (1,700 <a href="/wiki/Kilogram-force" title="Kilogram-force">kgf/cm<sup>2</sup></a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt-Nielsen_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-Nielsen-8">[8]</a></sup> poor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" title="Tensile strength">tensile strength</a> of 104–121 MPa, and a very low <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">shear stress</a> strength (51.6 MPa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> This means that bone resists pushing (compressional) stress well, resist pulling (tensional) stress less well, but only poorly resists shear stress (such as due to torsional loads). While bone is essentially <a href="/wiki/Brittleness" title="Brittleness">brittle</a>, bone does have a significant degree of <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">elasticity</a>, contributed chiefly by <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>.
</p><p>Mechanically, bones also have a special role in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hearing_(sense)" title="Hearing (sense)">hearing</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Ossicles" title="Ossicles">ossicles</a> are three small bones in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_ear" title="Middle ear">middle ear</a> which are involved in sound transduction.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthetic">Synthetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Synthetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The cancellous part of bones contain <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a>. Bone marrow produces blood cells in a process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hematopoiesis" title="Hematopoiesis">hematopoiesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Blood cells that are created in bone marrow include <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelets</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin200660–61_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin200660–61-44">[44]</a></sup> Progenitor cells such as the <a href="/wiki/Hematopoietic_stem_cell" title="Hematopoietic stem cell">hematopoietic stem cell</a> divide in a process called <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a> to produce precursor cells. These include precursors which eventually give rise to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_blood_cells" title="White blood cells">white blood cells</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erythroblast" title="Erythroblast">erythroblasts</a> which give rise to red blood cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin200660_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin200660-45">[45]</a></sup> Unlike red and white blood cells, created by mitosis, platelets are shed from very large cells called <a href="/wiki/Megakaryocyte" title="Megakaryocyte">megakaryocytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin200657_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin200657-46">[46]</a></sup> This process of progressive differentiation occurs within the bone marrow. After the cells are matured, they enter the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin200646_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin200646-47">[47]</a></sup> Every day, over 2.5 billion red blood cells and platelets, and 50–100 billion <a href="/wiki/Granulocyte" title="Granulocyte">granulocytes</a> are produced in this way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin200658_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin200658-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>As well as creating cells, bone marrow is also one of the major sites where defective or aged red blood cells are destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeakin200658_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeakin200658-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metabolic">Metabolic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Metabolic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Mineral storage – bones act as reserves of minerals important for the body, most notably <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Determined by the species, age, and the type of bone, bone cells make up to 15 percent of the bone. <a href="/wiki/Growth_factor" title="Growth factor">Growth factor</a> storage—mineralized bone matrix stores important growth factors such as <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>-like growth factors, transforming growth factor, <a href="/wiki/Bone_morphogenetic_protein" title="Bone morphogenetic protein">bone morphogenetic proteins</a> and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">Fat</a> storage – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marrow_adipose_tissue" title="Marrow adipose tissue">marrow adipose tissue</a> (MAT) acts as a storage reserve of <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">Acid</a>-<a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a> balance – bone buffers the blood against excessive <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> changes by absorbing or releasing <a href="/wiki/Alkali_salt" title="Alkali salt">alkaline salts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></li>
<li>Detoxification – bone tissues can also store <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a> and other foreign elements, removing them from the blood and reducing their effects on other tissues. These can later be gradually released for <a href="/wiki/Excretion" title="Excretion">excretion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">Endocrine</a> organ – bone controls <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> metabolism by releasing <a href="/wiki/Fibroblast_growth_factor_23" title="Fibroblast growth factor 23">fibroblast growth factor 23</a> (FGF-23), which acts on <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a> to reduce phosphate <a href="/wiki/Reabsorption" title="Reabsorption">reabsorption</a>. Bone cells also release a hormone called <a href="/wiki/Osteocalcin" title="Osteocalcin">osteocalcin</a>, which contributes to the regulation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_sugar" title="Blood sugar">blood sugar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Adipose_tissue" title="Adipose tissue">fat deposition</a>. Osteocalcin increases both the <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> secretion and sensitivity, in addition to boosting the number of <a href="/wiki/Beta_cell" title="Beta cell">insulin-producing cells</a> and reducing stores of fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li>Calcium balance – the process of bone resorption by the osteoclasts releases stored calcium into the systemic circulation and is an important process in regulating calcium balance. As bone formation actively <i>fixes</i> circulating calcium in its mineral form, removing it from the bloodstream, resorption actively <i>unfixes</i> it thereby increasing circulating calcium levels. These processes occur in tandem at site-specific locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Remodeling">Remodeling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Remodeling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bone_remodeling" title="Bone remodeling">Bone remodeling</a></div>
<p>Bone is constantly being created and replaced in a process known as <a href="/wiki/Bone_remodeling" title="Bone remodeling">remodeling</a>. This ongoing turnover of bone is a process of resorption followed by replacement of bone with little change in shape. This is accomplished through osteoblasts and osteoclasts. Cells are stimulated by a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paracrine" title="Paracrine">signals</a>, and together referred to as a remodeling unit. Approximately 10% of the skeletal mass of an adult is remodelled each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The purpose of remodeling is to regulate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcium_homeostasis" title="Calcium homeostasis">calcium homeostasis</a>, repair <a href="/wiki/Microdamage_in_bone" title="Microdamage in bone">microdamaged bones</a> from everyday stress, and to shape the skeleton during growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Repeated stress, such as weight-bearing <a href="/wiki/Exercise" title="Exercise">exercise</a> or bone healing, results in the bone thickening at the points of maximum stress (<a href="/wiki/Wolff%27s_law" title="Wolff's law">Wolff's law</a>). It has been hypothesized that this is a result of bone's <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectricity" title="Piezoelectricity">piezoelectric</a> properties, which cause bone to generate small electrical potentials under stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The action of osteoblasts and osteoclasts are controlled by a number of chemical <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> that either promote or inhibit the activity of the bone remodeling cells, controlling the rate at which bone is made, destroyed, or changed in shape. The cells also use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paracrine_signalling" title="Paracrine signalling">paracrine signalling</a> to control the activity of each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> For example, the rate at which osteoclasts resorb bone is inhibited by <a href="/wiki/Calcitonin" title="Calcitonin">calcitonin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osteoprotegerin" title="Osteoprotegerin">osteoprotegerin</a>. Calcitonin is produced by <a href="/wiki/Parafollicular_cell" title="Parafollicular cell">parafollicular cells</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_gland" title="Thyroid gland">thyroid gland</a>, and can bind to receptors on osteoclasts to directly inhibit osteoclast activity. Osteoprotegerin is secreted by osteoblasts and is able to bind RANK-L, inhibiting osteoclast stimulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boron_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boron-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Osteoblasts can also be stimulated to increase bone mass through increased secretion of <a href="/wiki/Osteoid" title="Osteoid">osteoid</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_inhibitor" title="Enzyme inhibitor">inhibiting</a> the ability of osteoclasts to break down <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osseous_tissue" title="Osseous tissue">osseous tissue</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Increased secretion of osteoid is stimulated by the secretion of <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormone</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pituitary" title="Pituitary">pituitary</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_hormone" title="Thyroid hormone">thyroid hormone</a> and the sex hormones (<a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Androgen" title="Androgen">androgens</a>). These hormones also promote increased secretion of osteoprotegerin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boron_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boron-62">[62]</a></sup> Osteoblasts can also be induced to secrete a number of <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a> that promote reabsorption of bone by stimulating osteoclast activity and differentiation from progenitor cells. <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">Vitamin D</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parathyroid_hormone" title="Parathyroid hormone">parathyroid hormone</a> and stimulation from osteocytes induce osteoblasts to increase secretion of RANK-<a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_6" title="Interleukin 6">interleukin 6</a>, which cytokines then stimulate increased reabsorption of bone by osteoclasts. These same compounds also increase secretion of <a href="/wiki/Macrophage_colony-stimulating_factor" title="Macrophage colony-stimulating factor">macrophage colony-stimulating factor</a> by osteoblasts, which promotes the differentiation of progenitor cells into osteoclasts, and decrease secretion of osteoprotegerin.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Volume">Volume</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Volume">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bone volume is determined by the rates of bone formation and bone resorption. Recent research has suggested that certain growth factors may work to locally alter bone formation by increasing osteoblast activity. Numerous bone-derived growth factors have been isolated and classified via bone cultures. These factors include insulin-like growth factors I and II, transforming growth factor-beta, fibroblast growth factor, platelet-derived growth factor, and bone morphogenetic proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ukpmc.ac.uk_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-ukpmc.ac.uk-63">[63]</a></sup> Evidence suggests that bone cells produce growth factors for extracellular storage in the bone matrix. The release of these growth factors from the bone matrix could cause the proliferation of osteoblast precursors. Essentially, bone growth factors may act as potential determinants of local bone formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ukpmc.ac.uk_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-ukpmc.ac.uk-63">[63]</a></sup> Research has suggested that cancellous bone volume in postmenopausal osteoporosis may be determined by the relationship between the total bone forming surface and the percent of surface resorption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid6114324_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid6114324-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Clinical_significance">Clinical significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Clinical significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bone_disease" title="Bone disease">Bone disease</a></div>
<p>A number of diseases can affect bone, including arthritis, fractures, infections, osteoporosis and tumors. Conditions relating to bone can be managed by a variety of doctors, including <a href="/wiki/Rheumatology" title="Rheumatology">rheumatologists</a> for joints, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthopedic" title="Orthopedic">orthopedic</a> surgeons, who may conduct surgery to fix broken bones. Other doctors, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rehabilitation_medicine" title="Rehabilitation medicine">rehabilitation specialists</a> may be involved in recovery, <a href="/wiki/Radiology" title="Radiology">radiologists</a> in interpreting the findings on imaging, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathologist" title="Pathologist">pathologists</a> in investigating the cause of the disease, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Family_doctor" title="Family doctor">family doctors</a> may play a role in preventing complications of bone disease such as osteoporosis.
</p><p>When a doctor sees a patient, a history and exam will be taken. Bones are then often imaged, called <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">radiography</a>. This might include <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a> <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a>, <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI_scan" title="MRI scan">MRI scan</a> and other imaging such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bone_scan" title="Bone scan">Bone scan</a>, which may be used to investigate cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101059–1062_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101059–1062-65">[65]</a></sup> Other tests such as a blood test for autoimmune markers may be taken, or a <a href="/wiki/Synovial_fluid" title="Synovial fluid">synovial fluid</a> aspirate may be taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101059–1062_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101059–1062-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fractures">Fractures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Fractures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xraymachine.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3504" data-file-width="2336" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Xraymachine.JPG/220px-Xraymachine.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Xraymachine.JPG/330px-Xraymachine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Xraymachine.JPG/440px-Xraymachine.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xraymachine.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">Radiography</a> used to identify possible <a href="/wiki/Bone_fracture" title="Bone fracture">bone fractures</a> after a knee injury</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bone_fracture" title="Bone fracture">Bone fracture</a></div>
<p>In normal bone, <a href="/wiki/Bone_fracture" title="Bone fracture">fractures</a> occur when there is significant force applied or repetitive trauma over a long time. Fractures can also occur when a bone is weakened, such as with osteoporosis, or when there is a structural problem, such as when the bone remodels excessively (such as <a href="/wiki/Paget%27s_disease_of_bone" title="Paget's disease of bone">Paget's disease</a>) or is the site of the growth of cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101068_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101068-66">[66]</a></sup> Common fractures include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrist_fracture" title="Wrist fracture">wrist fractures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hip_fracture" title="Hip fracture">hip fractures</a>, associated with <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">osteoporosis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebral_fracture" title="Vertebral fracture">vertebral fractures</a> associated with high-energy trauma and cancer, and fractures of long-bones. Not all fractures are painful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101068_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101068-66">[66]</a></sup> When serious, depending on the fractures type and location, complications may include <a href="/wiki/Flail_chest" title="Flail chest">flail chest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compartment_syndrome" title="Compartment syndrome">compartment syndromes</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fat_embolism" title="Fat embolism">fat embolism</a>.
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compound_fracture" title="Compound fracture">Compound fractures</a> involve the bone's penetration through the skin. Some complex fractures can be treated by the use of <a href="/wiki/Bone_grafting" title="Bone grafting">bone grafting</a> procedures that replace missing bone portions.
</p><p>Fractures and their underlying causes can be investigated by <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CT_scans" title="CT scans">CT scans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI" title="MRI">MRIs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101068_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101068-66">[66]</a></sup> Fractures are described by their location and shape, and several classification systems exist, depending on the location of the fracture. A common long bone fracture in children is a <a href="/wiki/Salter%E2%80%93Harris_fracture" title="Salter–Harris fracture">Salter–Harris fracture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> When fractures are managed, pain relief is often given, and the fractured area is often immobilised. This is to promote <a href="/wiki/Bone_healing" title="Bone healing">bone healing</a>. In addition, surgical measures such as <a href="/wiki/Internal_fixation" title="Internal fixation">internal fixation</a> may be used. Because of the immobilisation, people with fractures are often advised to undergo <a href="/wiki/Physical_medicine_and_rehabilitation" title="Physical medicine and rehabilitation">rehabilitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101068_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101068-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tumors">Tumors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Tumors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bone_tumor" title="Bone tumor">Bone tumor</a></div>
