<p>There are several types of tumor that can affect bone; examples of benign <a href="/wiki/Bone_tumor" title="Bone tumor">bone tumors</a> include <a href="/wiki/Osteoma" title="Osteoma">osteoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osteoid_osteoma" title="Osteoid osteoma">osteoid osteoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osteochondroma" title="Osteochondroma">osteochondroma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osteoblastoma" title="Osteoblastoma">osteoblastoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enchondroma" title="Enchondroma">enchondroma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giant-cell_tumor_of_bone" title="Giant-cell tumor of bone">giant-cell tumor of bone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aneurysmal_bone_cyst" title="Aneurysmal bone cyst">aneurysmal bone cyst</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Cancer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bone_metastases" title="Bone metastases">Bone metastases</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">Cancer</a> can arise in bone tissue, and bones are also a common site for other cancers to spread (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metastasise" title="Metastasise">metastasise</a>) to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101125_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101125-69">[69]</a></sup> Cancers that arise in bone are called "primary" cancers, although such cancers are rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101125_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101125-69">[69]</a></sup> Metastases within bone are "secondary" cancers, with the most common being <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thyroid_cancer" title="Thyroid cancer">thyroid cancer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kidney_cancer" title="Kidney cancer">kidney cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101125_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101125-69">[69]</a></sup> Secondary cancers that affect bone can either destroy bone (called a "<a href="/wiki/Lytic_cycle" title="Lytic cycle">lytic</a>" cancer) or create bone (a "<a href="/wiki/Sclerosis_(medicine)" title="Sclerosis (medicine)">sclerotic</a>" cancer). Cancers of the bone marrow inside the bone can also affect bone tissue, examples including <a href="/wiki/Leukemia" title="Leukemia">leukemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Multiple_myeloma" title="Multiple myeloma">multiple myeloma</a>. Bone may also be affected by cancers in other parts of the body. Cancers in other parts of the body may release <a href="/wiki/Parathyroid_hormone" title="Parathyroid hormone">parathyroid hormone</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parathyroid_hormone-related_peptide" title="Parathyroid hormone-related peptide">parathyroid hormone-related peptide</a>. This increases bone reabsorption, and can lead to bone fractures.
</p><p>Bone tissue that is destroyed or altered as a result of cancers is distorted, weakened, and more prone to fracture. This may lead to compression of the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>, destruction of the marrow resulting in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bruising" title="Bruising">bruising</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bleeding" title="Bleeding">bleeding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppression" title="Immunosuppression">immunosuppression</a>, and is one cause of bone pain. If the cancer is metastatic, then there might be other symptoms depending on the site of the original cancer. Some bone cancers can also be felt.
</p><p>Cancers of the bone are managed according to their type, their <a href="/wiki/Cancer_staging" title="Cancer staging">stage</a>, prognosis, and what symptoms they cause. Many primary cancers of bone are treated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiotherapy" title="Radiotherapy">radiotherapy</a>. Cancers of bone marrow may be treated with <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a>, and other forms of targeted therapy such as <a href="/wiki/Immunotherapy" title="Immunotherapy">immunotherapy</a> may be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101032_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101032-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Palliative_care" title="Palliative care">Palliative care</a>, which focuses on maximising a person's <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a>, may play a role in management, particularly if the likelihood of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-year_survival" title="5-year survival">survival within five years</a> is poor.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_painful_conditions">Other painful conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Other painful conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Osteomyelitis" title="Osteomyelitis">Osteomyelitis</a> is inflammation of the bone or bone marrow due to bacterial infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Osteomalacia" title="Osteomalacia">Osteomalacia</a> is a painful softening of adult bone caused by severe vitamin D deficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Osteogenesis_imperfecta" title="Osteogenesis imperfecta">Osteogenesis imperfecta</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Osteochondritis_dissecans" title="Osteochondritis dissecans">Osteochondritis dissecans</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ankylosing_spondylitis" title="Ankylosing spondylitis">Ankylosing spondylitis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Skeletal_fluorosis" title="Skeletal fluorosis">Skeletal fluorosis</a> is a bone disease caused by an excessive accumulation of <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> in the bones. In advanced cases, skeletal fluorosis damages bones and joints and is painful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Osteoporosis">Osteoporosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Osteoporosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">Osteoporosis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Osteoporosis_in_Bones.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Osteoporosis_in_Bones.jpg/220px-Osteoporosis_in_Bones.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Osteoporosis_in_Bones.jpg/330px-Osteoporosis_in_Bones.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Osteoporosis_in_Bones.jpg/440px-Osteoporosis_in_Bones.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Osteoporosis_in_Bones.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reduced bone mineral density in Osteoporosis (R), increasing the likelihood of fractures</div></div></div>
<p>Osteoporosis is a disease of bone where there is reduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bone_mineral_density" title="Bone mineral density">bone mineral density</a>, increasing the likelihood of <a href="/wiki/Bone_fracture" title="Bone fracture">fractures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101116–1121_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101116–1121-77">[77]</a></sup> Osteoporosis is defined in women by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> as a bone mineral density of 2.5 <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviations</a> below peak bone mass, relative to the age and sex-matched average. This density is measured using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dual_energy_X-ray_absorptiometry" title="Dual energy X-ray absorptiometry">dual energy X-ray absorptiometry</a> (DEXA), with the term "established osteoporosis" including the presence of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fragility_fracture" title="Fragility fracture">fragility fracture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO1994_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO1994-78">[78]</a></sup> Osteoporosis is most common in women after <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">menopause</a>, when it is called "postmenopausal osteoporosis", but may develop in men and premenopausal women in the presence of particular hormonal disorders and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronic_(medicine)" title="Chronic (medicine)">chronic</a> diseases or as a result of <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">smoking</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medications" title="Medications">medications</a>, specifically <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">glucocorticoids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101116–1121_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101116–1121-77">[77]</a></sup> Osteoporosis usually has no symptoms until a fracture occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101116–1121_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101116–1121-77">[77]</a></sup> For this reason, DEXA scans are often done in people with one or more risk factors, who have developed osteoporosis and are at risk of fracture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101116–1121_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson20101116–1121-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the most important risk factors for <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">osteoporosis</a> is <a href="/wiki/Ageing" title="Ageing">advanced age</a>.  Accumulation of oxidative <a href="/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring)" title="DNA damage (naturally occurring)">DNA damage</a> in <a href="/wiki/Osteocyte" title="Osteocyte">osteoblastic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osteoclast" title="Osteoclast">osteoclastic</a> cells appears to be a key factor in age-related osteoporosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Osteoporosis treatment includes advice to stop smoking, decrease alcohol consumption, exercise regularly, and have a healthy diet. <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">Calcium</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trace_mineral" title="Trace mineral">trace mineral</a> supplements may also be advised, as may <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">Vitamin D</a>. When medication is used, it may include <a href="/wiki/Bisphosphonate" title="Bisphosphonate">bisphosphonates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strontium_ranelate" title="Strontium ranelate">Strontium ranelate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hormone_replacement_therapy" title="Hormone replacement therapy">hormone replacement therapy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2010_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2010-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Osteopathic_medicine">Osteopathic medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Osteopathic medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Osteopathic_medicine_in_the_United_States" title="Osteopathic medicine in the United States">Osteopathic medicine in the United States</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osteopathic_medicine" title="Osteopathic medicine">Osteopathic medicine</a> is a school of medical thought originally developed based on the idea of the link between the musculoskeletal system and overall health, but now very similar to mainstream medicine. As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, over 77,000 physicians <a href="/wiki/Osteopathic_medicine_in_the_United_States" title="Osteopathic medicine in the United States">in the United States</a> are trained in osteopathic medical schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Osteology">Osteology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Osteology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paleopathology;_Human_femurs_from_Roman_period,_Tell_Fara_Wellcome_L0008764.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1408" data-file-width="1302" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Paleopathology%3B_Human_femurs_from_Roman_period%2C_Tell_Fara_Wellcome_L0008764.jpg/220px-Paleopathology%3B_Human_femurs_from_Roman_period%2C_Tell_Fara_Wellcome_L0008764.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Paleopathology%3B_Human_femurs_from_Roman_period%2C_Tell_Fara_Wellcome_L0008764.jpg/330px-Paleopathology%3B_Human_femurs_from_Roman_period%2C_Tell_Fara_Wellcome_L0008764.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Paleopathology%3B_Human_femurs_from_Roman_period%2C_Tell_Fara_Wellcome_L0008764.jpg/440px-Paleopathology%3B_Human_femurs_from_Roman_period%2C_Tell_Fara_Wellcome_L0008764.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paleopathology;_Human_femurs_from_Roman_period,_Tell_Fara_Wellcome_L0008764.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Human femurs and humerus from Roman period, with evidence of healed <a href="/wiki/Bone_fracture" title="Bone fracture">fractures</a></div></div></div>
<p>The study of bones and teeth is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Osteology" title="Osteology">osteology</a>. It is frequently used in <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archeology" title="Archeology">archeology</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forensics" title="Forensics">forensic science</a> for a variety of tasks. This can include determining the nutritional, health, age or injury status of the individual the bones were taken from. Preparing fleshed bones for these types of studies can involve the process of <a href="/wiki/Maceration_(bone)" title="Maceration (bone)">maceration</a>.
</p><p>Typically anthropologists and archeologists study <a href="/wiki/Bone_tool" title="Bone tool">bone tools</a> made by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_neanderthalensis" title="Homo neanderthalensis">Homo neanderthalensis</a></i>. Bones can serve a number of uses such as projectile points or artistic pigments, and can also be made from external bones such as <a href="/wiki/Antler" title="Antler">antlers</a>.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Other animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bird_anatomy" title="Bird anatomy">Bird anatomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">Exoskeleton</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fluwor%C3%B4ze_egzostozes1-800h.jpg"><img alt="knobby hoofed leg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Fluwor%C3%B4ze_egzostozes1-800h.jpg/180px-Fluwor%C3%B4ze_egzostozes1-800h.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Fluwor%C3%B4ze_egzostozes1-800h.jpg/270px-Fluwor%C3%B4ze_egzostozes1-800h.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Fluwor%C3%B4ze_egzostozes1-800h.jpg/360px-Fluwor%C3%B4ze_egzostozes1-800h.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fluwor%C3%B4ze_egzostozes1-800h.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Skeletal_fluorosis" title="Skeletal fluorosis">Skeletal fluorosis</a> in a cow's leg, due to industrial contamination</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bird_leg_and_pelvic_girdle_skeleton_EN.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Bird_leg_and_pelvic_girdle_skeleton_EN.gif/200px-Bird_leg_and_pelvic_girdle_skeleton_EN.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Bird_leg_and_pelvic_girdle_skeleton_EN.gif/300px-Bird_leg_and_pelvic_girdle_skeleton_EN.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Bird_leg_and_pelvic_girdle_skeleton_EN.gif/400px-Bird_leg_and_pelvic_girdle_skeleton_EN.gif 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bird_leg_and_pelvic_girdle_skeleton_EN.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Leg and pelvic girdle bones of bird</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">Bird</a> skeletons are very lightweight. Their bones are smaller and thinner, to aid flight. Among mammals, <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a> come closest to birds in terms of bone density, suggesting that small dense bones are a flight adaptation. Many bird bones have little marrow due to them being hollow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSPB-BirdBoneDensity_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-RSPB-BirdBoneDensity-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>A bird's <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">beak</a> is primarily made of bone as projections of the <a href="/wiki/Mandible" title="Mandible">mandibles</a> which are covered in <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a>.
</p><p>Some bones, primarily formed separately in subcutaneous tissues, include headgears (such as bony core of horns, antlers, ossicones), osteoderm, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Os_penis" title="Os penis">os penis</a>/ <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Os_clitoris" title="Os clitoris">os clitoris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Antler" title="Antler">antlers</a> are composed of bone which is an unusual example of bone being outside the skin of the animal once the velvet is shed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10321994_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10321994-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The extinct predatory fish <i><a href="/wiki/Dunkleosteus" title="Dunkleosteus">Dunkleosteus</a></i> had sharp edges of hard exposed bone along its jaws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>The proportion of cortical bone that is 80% in the human skeleton may be much lower in other animals, especially in <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">marine mammals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_turtles" title="Marine turtles">marine turtles</a>, or in various <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> <a href="/wiki/Marine_reptile" title="Marine reptile">marine reptiles</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Ichthyosaur" title="Ichthyosaur">ichthyosaurs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buff&amp;Maz90_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buff&amp;Maz90-87">[87]</a></sup> among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZL11_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZL11-88">[88]</a></sup> This proportion can vary quickly in evolution; it often increases in early stages of returns to an aquatic lifestyle, as seen in early <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">pinnipeds</a>, among others. It subsequently decreases in pelagic taxa, which typically acquire spongy bone, but aquatic taxa that live in shallow water can retain very thick, <a href="/wiki/Pachyostosis" title="Pachyostosis">pachyostotic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Osteosclerosis" title="Osteosclerosis">osteosclerotic</a>, or pachyosteosclerotic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> bones, especially if they move slowly, like <a href="/wiki/Sirenia" title="Sirenia">sea cows</a>. In some cases, even marine taxa that had acquired spongy bone can revert to thicker, compact bones if they become adapted to live in shallow water, or in <a href="/wiki/Hypersaline_lake" title="Hypersaline lake">hypersaline</a> (denser) water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many animals, particularly <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a>, practice <a href="/wiki/Osteophagy" title="Osteophagy">osteophagy</a>—the eating of bones. This is presumably carried out in order to replenish lacking <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a>.
</p><p>Many bone diseases that affect humans also affect other vertebrates—an example of one disorder is skeletal fluorosis.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bone&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bones_of_cattle_on_a_farm_in_Namibia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3239" data-file-width="4883" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Bones_of_cattle_on_a_farm_in_Namibia.jpg/220px-Bones_of_cattle_on_a_farm_in_Namibia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Bones_of_cattle_on_a_farm_in_Namibia.jpg/330px-Bones_of_cattle_on_a_farm_in_Namibia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Bones_of_cattle_on_a_farm_in_Namibia.jpg/440px-Bones_of_cattle_on_a_farm_in_Namibia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bones_of_cattle_on_a_farm_in_Namibia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bones of slaughtered <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Farm" title="Farm">farm</a> in <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Bones from slaughtered animals have a number of uses. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_times" title="Prehistoric times">prehistoric times</a>, they have been used for making <a href="/wiki/Bone_tool" title="Bone tool">bone tools</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> They have further been used in <a href="/wiki/Bone_carving" title="Bone carving">bone carving</a>, already important in <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_art" title="Prehistoric art">prehistoric art</a>, and also in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_time" title="Modern time">modern time</a> as crafting materials for <a href="/wiki/Button" title="Button">buttons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bead" title="Bead">beads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Handle" title="Handle">handles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bobbin" title="Bobbin">bobbins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Napier%27s_bones" title="Napier's bones">calculation aids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Head_nut" title="Head nut">head nuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dice" title="Dice">dice</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poker_chip" title="Poker chip">poker chips</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pick-up_sticks" title="Pick-up sticks">pick-up sticks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arrow" title="Arrow">arrows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scrimshaw" title="Scrimshaw">scrimshaw</a>, ornaments, etc.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bone_glue" title="Bone glue">Bone glue</a> can be made by prolonged boiling of ground or cracked bones, followed by filtering and evaporation to thicken the resulting fluid. Historically once important, bone glue and other animal glues today have only a few specialized uses, such as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiques_restoration" title="Antiques restoration">antiques restoration</a>. Essentially the same process, with further refinement, thickening and drying, is used to make <a href="/wiki/Gelatin" title="Gelatin">gelatin</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Broth" title="Broth">Broth</a> is made by simmering several ingredients for a long time, traditionally including bones.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bone_char" title="Bone char">Bone char</a>, a porous, black, granular material primarily used for <a href="/wiki/Filtration" title="Filtration">filtration</a> and also as a black <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a>, is produced by <a href="/wiki/Charring" title="Charring">charring</a> mammal bones.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">Oracle bone script</a> was a writing system used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">Ancient China</a> based on inscriptions in bones. Its name originates from oracle bones, which were mainly ox clavicle. The Ancient Chinese (mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>), would write their questions on the <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone" title="Oracle bone">oracle bone</a>, and burn the bone, and where the bone cracked would be the answer for the questions.
</p><p>To <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/point_the_bone" title="wikt:point the bone">point the bone</a> at someone is considered bad luck in some cultures, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_aborigines" title="Australian aborigines">Australian aborigines</a>, such as by the <a href="/wiki/Kurdaitcha#Bone_pointing" title="Kurdaitcha">Kurdaitcha</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Furcula" title="Furcula">wishbones</a> of fowl have been used for <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a>, and are still customarily used in a tradition to determine which one of two people pulling on either prong of the bone may make a wish.
</p><p>Various cultures throughout history have adopted the custom of shaping an infant's head by the practice of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_cranial_deformation" title="Artificial cranial deformation">artificial cranial deformation</a>. A widely practised custom in China was that of <a href="/wiki/Foot_binding" title="Foot binding">foot binding</a> to limit the normal growth of the foot.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Schmidt-Nielsen-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Schmidt-Nielsen_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Schmidt-Nielsen_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFSchmidt-Nielsen,_Knut1984"><a href="/wiki/Knut_Schmidt-Nielsen" title="Knut Schmidt-Nielsen">Schmidt-Nielsen, Knut</a> (1984). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/scalingwhyisanim0000schm/page/6" rel="nofollow"><i>Scaling: Why Is Animal Size So Important?</i></a>. </div></td></tr><tr><th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%"><a href="/wiki/Forearm" title="Forearm">Forearm</a></th><td class="navbox-list-with-group navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0"><div style="padding:0 0.25em"></div><table class="nowraplinks navbox-subgroup" style="border-spacing:0"><tbody><tr><th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%"><a href="/wiki/Radius_(bone)" title="Radius (bone)">Radius</a></th><td class="navbox-list-with-group navbox-list navbox-even" style="width:100%;padding:0"><div style="padding:0 0.25em">
